                .addSize([0, 0], [640, 480])
                .build()

        gptSubPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.subPage ? digitalData.page.subPage : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

        googletag.pubads()
            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/anie.202400539')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        googletag.defineSlot($GooglePublisherCategory, 'fluid', 'advert-leaderboard')
            .defineSizeMapping(sizes.leaderboard)

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '534b40d4-110a-41fe-bc9f-7138c7085e7c'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d249474985" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>The weakly solvating electrolyte is formulated with 2 -methyltetrahydrofuran and tetrahydrofuran to decrease the ion-dipole interactions. Its anion-enhanced solvation structures facilitate stable sodium storage in hard carbon anode at ultra-low temperatures. The full cell paired with NVP cathode operates over 250 stable cycles with high capacity retention of ~100 % at 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup> at −40 °C.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1248729b-c4c2-4236-b8d7-e63a898f4d7f/anie202400539-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/1248729b-c4c2-4236-b8d7-e63a898f4d7f/anie202400539-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e398c62f-7bed-4c48-8f8b-3d7fd1727b61/anie202400539-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1248729b-c4c2-4236-b8d7-e63a898f4d7f/anie202400539-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d249474988" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are recognized as promising energy storage devices. However, they suffer from rapid capacity decay at ultra-low temperatures due to high Na<sup>+</sup> desolvation energy barrier and unstable solid electrolyte interphase (SEI). Herein, a weakly solvating electrolyte (WSE) with decreased ion-dipole interactions is designed for stable sodium storage in hard carbon (HC) anode at ultra-low temperatures. 2-methyltetrahydrofuran with low solvating power is incorporated into tetrahydrofuran to regulate the interactions between Na<sup>+</sup> and solvents. The reduced Na<sup>+</sup>-dipole interactions facilitate more anionic coordination in the first solvation sheath, which consistently maintains anion-enhanced solvation structures from room to low temperatures to promote inorganic-rich SEI formation. These enable WSE with a low freezing point of −83.3 °C and faster Na<sup>+</sup> desolvation kinetics. The HC anode thus affords reversible capacities of 243.2 and 205.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup> at −40 and −60 °C, respectively, and the full cell of HC||Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> yields an extended lifespan over 250 cycles with high capacity retention of ~100 % at −40 °C. This work sheds new lights on the ion-dipole regulation for ultra-low temperature SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202400539-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202400539-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered a promising grid-scale energy storage technology due to abundant Na resources and their low cost.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400539-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400539-bib-0001_R_d249474977e485" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1">1</a></sup></span> However, the wide-temperature operation of SIBs is restricted by the inadaptability of electrolytes and unstable anodes at low temperatures. This is significantly related to the Na<sup>+</sup> desolvation kinetics and ion transport across the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI).<span><sup><a href="#anie202400539-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400539-bib-0006_R_d249474977e490" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2">2</a></sup></span> Although carbonate esters have been widely applied in the electrolytes of SIBs, the polar carbonyl groups of solvents exhibit excessive interactions with Na<sup>+</sup>, leading to intense solvent coordination in the solvation sheath. The resultant strong Na<sup>+</sup>-dipole interactions give rise to a low freezing point of the carbonate ester-based electrolytes, leading to unsatisfactory rate capability at low temperatures.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400539-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400539-bib-0011_R_d249474977e498" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3">3</a></sup></span> The ether-based electrolytes have attracted considerable attention due to their low viscosity and better reductive stability compared with carbonate esters.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400539-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400539-bib-0016_R_d249474977e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4">4</a></sup></span> However, the strong Na<sup>+</sup>- solvent coordination also causes intensive Na<sup>+</sup>-dipole interactions, which results in sluggish Na<sup>+</sup> desolvation and organic-rich SEI formation at low temperatures. The unstable SEI hinders interfacial ion transport and continuously induces electrolyte decomposition, leading to attenuated capacity and inferior cycling stability at low temperatures.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400539-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400539-bib-0020_R_d249474977e510" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5">5</a></sup></span> These urge appropriate regulation of ion-dipole interactions and solvation structures to improve the interfacial kinetics for high-performance SIBs under low temperatures.</p>
         
         <p>Incorporating anions into the inner solvation sheath is beneficial to reducing the ion-dipole interaction, which results from the weakened cation-solvent coordination. Anion-enhanced solvation structures are characterized by favorable Na<sup>+</sup> desolvation kinetics and effectively regulate the SEI components to improve battery performance.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400539-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400539-bib-0024_R_d249474977e518" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">6</a></sup></span> Typically, high-concentration electrolytes (HCEs) and localized high-concentration electrolytes (LHCEs) have been confirmed to construct anion-enhanced solvation structures. The preferential anionic decomposition promotes inorganic-rich SEI formation on the anode. These suppress solvent decomposition and result in better cycling stability.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400539-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400539-bib-0025_R_d249474977e521" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nature">7</a></sup></span> However, HCEs and LHCEs suffer from insufficient wetting capability, high viscosity, and high cost of salts or diluents.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400539-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400539-bib-0026_R_d249474977e524" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8">8</a></sup></span> An emerging electrolyte system, namely the weakly solvating electrolytes (WSE), has been employed to tune the solvating behavior of anions. Anion-derived SEI and Na<sup>+</sup> desolvation can be regulated in dilute electrolytes without adding salts and diluents.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400539-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400539-bib-0029_R_d249474977e530" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9">9</a></sup></span> However, the practical application of WSE at low temperatures is limited by its high freezing point, which originates from the strong ion-dipole interactions. To date, a comprehensive study of WSE for low-temperature SIBs is rarely reported, and its mechanistic understanding remains elusive. It remains challenging to formulate suitable WSE to enable the stable operation of SIBs at extremely low temperatures.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, 2-methyltetrahydrofuran (2MeTHF) is introduced into the WSE of sodium hexafluorophosphate/tetrahydrofuran (NaPF<sub>6</sub>/THF) to regulate the ion-dipole interactions for stable HC anodes in SIBs at ultra-low temperatures. The decreased cation-solvent binding strength facilitates more anions to participate in the solvation sheath, leading to weakened Na<sup>+</sup>-dipole interactions. Anion-enhanced solvation structures promote Na<sup>+</sup> desolvation kinetics and induce preferential anionic decomposition on the anode. The obtained inorganic-rich SEI reduces the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion barrier and suppresses electrolyte decomposition. These enable the HC anode with high reversible capacity and excellent cycling stability at ultra-low temperatures.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202400539-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202400539-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202400539-sec-0003">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202400539-sec-0003-title"> Physical properties and solvation structure</h3>
            
            <p>2MeTHF, featured with low polarity and dielectric constant, is used as the cosolvent within 1.0 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in a 5 : 5 v/v mixture of THF/2MeTHF (denoted as N-mixTHF). Two control groups are prepared with 1.0 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>/2MeTHF (denoted as N-2MeTHF) and 1.0 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>/THF (denoted as N-THF). Raman spectroscopy was applied to investigate the Na<sup>+</sup> coordination environment in the different electrolytes, as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202400539-fig-0001">1a</a> and Figure S1. The two signals at 913.4 and 919.6 cm<sup>−1</sup> are assigned to the ring breathing mode of C−O−C in free THF and 2MeTHF, respectively.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400539-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400539-bib-0033_R_d249474977e572" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">10</a></sup></span> With the addition of NaPF<sub>6</sub>, the coordinated THF and 2MeTHF peaks in N-mixTHF negatively shift from 914.3 and 920.3 cm<sup>−1</sup> to 913.7 and 919.8 cm<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, suggesting the weakened Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent interactions in N-mixTHF, as shown in Figure S2 and Table S1. The peak at 738.8 cm<sup>−1</sup> is ascribed to the stretching vibration of solvated PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>, which shifts negatively from 761.8 cm<sup>−1</sup> compared to solid NaPF<sub>6</sub> salt.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400539-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400539-bib-0034_R_d249474977e593" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">11</a></sup></span> The proportions of free/coordinated PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> in different electrolytes are calculated based on the Raman results, as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202400539-fig-0001">1b</a> and Figure S3. The percentage of contact ion pairs (CIPs) and aggregates (AGGs) are estimated to be 91.9 %, 89.9 %, and 85.9 % in N-mixTHF, N-2MeTHF, and N-THF, respectively. This demonstrates more PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> coordination in the solvation sheath of N-mixTHF, indicating weakened ion-dipole interactions between Na<sup>+</sup> and solvents in N-mixTHF.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202400539-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4b46884b-368d-48cd-9873-f1c8d6a0eca5/anie202400539-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/4b46884b-368d-48cd-9873-f1c8d6a0eca5/anie202400539-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9eabb6e2-0d91-45a9-9eb7-a83f5e4ef2f2/anie202400539-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4b46884b-368d-48cd-9873-f1c8d6a0eca5/anie202400539-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202400539-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202400539" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>(a) Raman spectra of N-mixTHF, N-2MeTHF, and N-THF, and (b) ratio of different coordinated structures. (c) <sup>17</sup>O NMR and (d) FTIR spectra of three electrolytes. (e) DSC curves and (f) calculated <i>σ<sub>Na</sub></i><sup><i>+</i></sup> at the temperature range from 50 °C to −80 °C. <i>T<sub>s</sub></i>: salt precipitation temperature.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p><sup>17</sup>O nuclear magnetic resonance (<sup>17</sup>O NMR) was further performed to reveal the Na<sup>+</sup> solvation configurations in the different electrolytes. The <sup>17</sup>O peak of THF in N-mixTHF shifts from 15.4 to 15.9 ppm related to N-THF. Similarly, the <sup>17</sup>O peak of 2MeTHF in N-mixTHF positively shifts from 43.9 to 44.2 ppm compared to N-2MeTHF, as illustrated in Figure <a href="#anie202400539-fig-0001">1c</a> and Figure S4. These demonstrate the decreased binding strength between Na<sup>+</sup> and THF/2MeTHF solvents in N-mixTHF, which is confirmed by the <sup>23</sup>Na and <sup>19</sup>F NMR spectra in Figure S5–S6.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400539-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400539-bib-0035_R_d249474977e665" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Sci.">12</a></sup></span> The coordination state of the Na<sup>+</sup> solvation structures was further analyzed by Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR) in Figure <a href="#anie202400539-fig-0001">1d</a> and Figure S7. The characteristic peaks at 1066.5 and 1089.3 cm<sup>−1</sup> are assigned to the C−O−C stretching vibration in pure THF and 2MeTHF solvents, respectively.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400539-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400539-bib-0036_R_d249474977e676" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Mater.">13</a></sup></span> When NaPF<sub>6</sub> is dissolved in the THF and 2MeTHF, the new peaks appear at 1052.2 and 1079.3 cm<sup>−1</sup> in N-THF and N-2MeTHF, which is attributed to the strong Na<sup>+</sup>-O<sub>THF</sub> and Na<sup>+</sup>-O<sub>2MeTHF</sub> interactions. The Na<sup>+</sup>-O peaks in N-mixTHF present a redshift of 2.1 and 7.9 cm<sup>−1</sup> compared with N-THF and N-2MeTHF, respectively. This indicates the reduced electron density around Na<sup>+</sup>, demonstrating the weakened ion-dipole interactions between Na<sup>+</sup> and THF/2MeTHF solvents in N-mixTHF.</p>
            
            <p>Electrolytes characterized by a low freezing point and a high boiling point are essential for battery operation under extreme temperature conditions. The phase transition of the three electrolytes is investigated by differential scanning calorimetry (DSC), as displayed in Figure <a href="#anie202400539-fig-0001">1e</a>. Benefiting from the weakened ion-dipole interactions, the N-mixTHF has the lowest freezing point of −83.3 °C compared to −42.8 °C and −28.3 °C in N-2MeTHF and N-THF, respectively. Low-temperature Raman spectroscopy was conducted to study the temperature adaptability of different electrolytes, as shown in Figure S8. The emerging peak at 755.5 cm<sup>−1</sup> in the N-THF and 2MeTHF at −40 and −60 °C, respectively, is assigned to precipitated solid NaPF<sub>6</sub>. In contrast, no precipitated NaPF<sub>6</sub> peak appears in the N-mixTHF even at −80 °C due to its lower freezing point, as revealed by optical photographs in Figure S9–S13. This demonstrates the decreased intermolecular interactions enabled by ion-dipole regulation in N-mixTHF, which is significant for electrolyte application at low temperatures.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400539-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400539-bib-0037_R_d249474977e712" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Nat. Commun.">14</a></sup></span></p>
            
            <p>The Na-ion conductivity (<i>σ</i><sub><i>Na+</i></sub>) of the electrolyte was calculated based on the electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements at different temperatures, as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202400539-fig-0001">1f</a>, Figure S14, and Table S2. The ionic conductivities of the three electrolytes all decrease sharply with declined temperature due to the significantly increased viscosity at low temperatures.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400539-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400539-bib-0038_R_d249474977e725" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">15</a></sup></span> Notably, the ionic conductivities of N-mixTHF, N-2MeTHF, and N-THF are calculated to be 0.87, 0.01, and 0.01 mS cm<sup>−1</sup> at −60 °C, respectively. This can be attributed to the NaPF<sub>6</sub> precipitation in the N-2MeTHF and N-THF, which is consistent with the DSC and low-temperature Raman results. Linear sweeping voltammetry (LSV) and <sup>1</sup>H NMR measurements were performed to investigate the oxidation stability of the three electrolytes in Figure S15 and S16a. N-mixTHF exhibits a wider electrochemical window above 4.0 V compared to N-2MeTHF and N-THF at 25 and −40 °C. The unchanged <sup>1</sup>H NMR spectra of N-mixTHF after fifity cycles in Na||HC cells suggest negligible solvent decomposition. Na metal was soaked into N-mixTHF to evaluate its chemical stability, in which no color changes were observed for Na metal and N-mixTHF in Figure S16b. These indicate the high chemical and electrochemical stability of N-mixTHF.</p>
            
            <p>Classical molecular dynamics (MD) simulations were performed to reveal the Na<sup>+</sup> solvation configurations and coordination environments of N-mixTHF, N-2MeTHF, and N-THF from 25 to −60 °C.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400539-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400539-bib-0039_R_d249474977e742" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">16</a></sup></span> Radial distribution functions (RDF) display the distributions of solvents and anions around Na<sup>+</sup> in the different electrolytes, as displayed in Figure <a href="#anie202400539-fig-0002">2a</a>-2c, Figure S17–S19, and Table S3. The coordination number (CN) of Na−F in N-mixTHF, N-2MeTHF, and N-THF are 3.89, 4.10, and 3.74 at 25 °C, respectively. This indicates that anion-derived Na<sup>+</sup> solvation structures are dominant in all three electrolytes at room temperature. However, the CN of solvents sharply increases in N-THF and N-2MeTHF as the temperature decreases to −40 °C, leading to solvent-enhanced solvation structures. On the one hand, the CN of the THF is increased from 2.41 to 3.72 in N-THF, as well as that of the 2MeTHF from 1.16 to 2.13 in N-2MeTHF in Figure S20. On the other hand, the CN of the 2MeTHF in N-mixTHF is almost unchanged from 0.35 to 0.33. THF coordination in N-mixTHF exhibits a slighter increase from 1.52 to 2.70 compared to that in N-THF. This suggests that the addition of 2MeTHF decreases Na<sup>+</sup>-dipole interactions with solvents and maintains the anion-dominated solvation structures in N-mixTHF over a wide range of temperatures.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202400539-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9ac83941-5b90-4b87-8633-191db6c32826/anie202400539-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/9ac83941-5b90-4b87-8633-191db6c32826/anie202400539-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/77405451-f8d1-4226-b612-9de93e727803/anie202400539-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9ac83941-5b90-4b87-8633-191db6c32826/anie202400539-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202400539-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202400539" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>(a–c) RDF of Na<sup>+</sup> solvation configurations obtained from MD simulations for electrolytes at −40 °C. (d-f) MD snapshots at −40 °C. Light coral, turquoise, dark blue, and red molecules represent coordinated THF, 2MeTHF, PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>, and Na<sup>+</sup>. (g-i) Typical solvation structures given by MD simulations in different electrolytes at −40 °C. Blue points on the bond paths represent the BCP sites. (j) Calculated electron density of BCP for Na<sup>+</sup>-O bond in three electrolytes. (I) O in N-THF, (II) O of THF in N-mixTHF, (III) O in N-2MeTHF, and (IV) O of 2MeTHF in N-mixTHF. (k) Schematics of different Na<sup>+</sup> desolvation at HC anode surface.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>The snapshots of the first solvation sheath at different simulated temperatures are displayed in Figure <a href="#anie202400539-fig-0002">2d</a>–<a href="#anie202400539-fig-0002">2f</a> and Figure S21–S23. N-mixTHF has more CIPs and AGGs structures compared to N-2MeTHF and N-THF, demonstrating enhanced cation-anion interaction, as evidenced by the Raman and NMR analysis in Figure <a href="#anie202400539-fig-0001">1a</a>–<a href="#anie202400539-fig-0001">1c</a>. The specific Na<sup>+</sup> solvation configurations and corresponding topological analysis in the varied electrolytes are exhibited in Figure <a href="#anie202400539-fig-0002">2g</a>–<a href="#anie202400539-fig-0002">2i</a>. The average Na<sup>+</sup>-O<sub>THF</sub> distance (2.53 Å) and Na<sup>+</sup>-O<sub>2MeTHF</sub> distance (2.81 Å) in N-mixTHF are larger than those in N-THF (2.42 Å) and N-2MeTHF (2.47 Å), suggesting the weakened Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent coordination in N-mixTHF. Topological electron density analysis was performed to compare the Na<sup>+</sup>-dipole interaction in the different electrolytes. The bond critical points (BCPs) are utilized to identify electron density distributions and bonding characteristics in the solvation structures.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400539-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400539-bib-0040_R_d249474977e826" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">17</a></sup></span> The BCPs of the Na<sup>+</sup>-O<sub>solvent</sub> bonds and their corresponding electron density (<i>ρ<sub>e</sub></i>) in different solvation configurations are displayed in Figure <a href="#anie202400539-fig-0002">2j</a> and Table S4. The <i>ρ<sub>e</sub></i> of Na<sup>+</sup>-O<sub>THF</sub> and Na<sup>+</sup>-O<sub>2MeTHF</sub> in N-mixTHF is 0.05758 and 0.01866, respectively, which is lower than that of Na<sup>+</sup>-O<sub>THF</sub> in N-THF (0.07235) and Na<sup>+</sup>-O<sub>2MeTHF</sub> in N-2MeTHF (0.04561). The lower <i>ρ<sub>e</sub></i> of Na<sup>+</sup>-O<sub>solvent</sub> suggests the weakened ion-dipole interactions between Na<sup>+</sup> and solvents. This leads to more anionic participation in the solvation sheath and improves the Na<sup>+</sup> desolvation kinetics, as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202400539-fig-0002">2k</a>.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202400539-sec-0004">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202400539-sec-0004-title"> Interfacial chemistry and kinetics</h3>
            
            <p>The thickness of the SEI on the HC anode at −40 °C was measured by high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM), as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202400539-fig-0003">3a</a>–<a href="#anie202400539-fig-0003">3c</a>. The SEI on the HC anode in N-mixTHF is uniform with a thickness of 30 nm. However, SEI in N-THF and N-2MeTHF are uneven and they have higher thicknesses of 50 and 70 nm, respectively. Moreover, N-mixTHF consistently keeps a thinner and more uniform SEI from 25 to −60 °C, compared to N-THF and N-2MeTHF, as shown in Figure S24–S26. This demonstrates that the weakened ion-dipole interactions in N-mixTHF contribute to the stable interface on the HC anode. In-depth X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was employed to characterize SEI components in Figure <a href="#anie202400539-fig-0003">3d</a>–<a href="#anie202400539-fig-0003">3f</a>. The two peaks located at 685.6 and 689.4 eV in the F 1s spectra can be assigned to the NaF and organic P−F species, respectively.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400539-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400539-bib-0041_R_d249474977e899" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">18</a></sup></span> The higher intensity of Na−F peak in the N-mixTHF implies the inorganic-rich SEI formation on the HC anode. The SEI in N-mixTHF is featured with high F content (14.5 % to 10.8 %) from 25 to −60 °C, as shown in Figure S27–S32. By contrast, the F content of SEI in N-THF and N-2MeTHF dramatically reduced from 12.4 % to 1.1 % and 11.4 % to 5.2 %, respectively, as displayed in Figure S33–S42. The obtained inorganic-rich SEI over a wide temperature range benefits from the anion-dominated solvation structures in N-mixTHF, which agrees with the MD simulations and XPS spectra.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202400539-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/87c9a714-4341-4370-8c6e-ba78bc522194/anie202400539-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/87c9a714-4341-4370-8c6e-ba78bc522194/anie202400539-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9bd98014-cc7c-48bb-b85b-4abe7225fcff/anie202400539-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/87c9a714-4341-4370-8c6e-ba78bc522194/anie202400539-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202400539-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202400539" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>HRTEM images of HC anodes after three cycles in (a) N-mixTHF, (b) N-2MeTHF, and (c) N-THF at −40 °C, and (d-f) their respective XPS spectra of F 1s with different sputtering times. (g) HOMO and LUMO energy of different solvation structures: (I) Na<sup>+</sup>[PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>]<sub>2</sub>[THF]<sub>4</sub>, (II) Na<sup>+</sup>[PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>]<sub>3</sub>[2MeTHF]<sub>3</sub>, (III) Na<sup>+</sup>[PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>]<sub>4</sub>[2MeTHF][THF]<sub>3</sub> and (IV) Na<sup>+</sup>[PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>]<sub>4</sub>[2MeTHF]<sub>2</sub>. (h) Mechanism of Na<sup>+</sup>-dipole regulation on formation of inorganic-rich SEI.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>The highest occupied molecular orbital (HOMO) and lowest unoccupied molecular orbital (LUMO) energies of the Na<sup>+</sup>-complex are calculated by density functional theory (DFT) in Figure <a href="#anie202400539-fig-0003">3g</a>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400539-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400539-bib-0042_R_d249474977e974" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Comput. Chem.">19</a></sup></span> The Na<sup>+</sup>[PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>]<sub>4</sub>[2MeTHF][THF]<sub>3</sub> solvate presents the lowest LUMO energy of −0.14 eV, compared to Na<sup>+</sup>[PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>]<sub>2</sub>[THF]<sub>4</sub> (−0.13 eV), Na<sup>+</sup>[PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>]<sub>3</sub>[2MeTHF]<sub>3</sub> (0.14 eV) and Na<sup>+</sup>[PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>]<sub>4</sub>[2MeTHF]<sub>2</sub> (0.35 eV). Solvates in N-mixTHF thus tend to accept electrons for prior reduction, which promotes the preferential decomposition of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> on the HC anode. The resultant SEI is enriched with NaF and suppresses the continuous solvent decomposition. The regulated solvation structures and SEI construction are schematically shown in Figure <a href="#anie202400539-fig-0003">3h</a>. The addition of 2MeTHF weakens the Na<sup>+</sup>-dipole interactions and increases the Na<sup>+</sup>-anion binding strength in WSE, which leads to a reduced electrolyte freezing point from −28.3 to −83.3 °C. The enhanced anion coordination in the solvation structures promotes anionic decomposition on the HC anode. This finally results in uniform and robust inorganic-rich SEI formation in N-mixTHF over a wide range of temperatures.</p>
            
            <p>The Na<sup>+</sup> desolvation and diffusion kinetics in the SEI are evaluated in Na||Na symmetric batteries based on temperature-dependent EIS, as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202400539-fig-0004">4a</a> and Figure S43. The activation energy (<i>E</i><sub>a</sub>) of Na<sup>+</sup> transport in the SEI (<i>Ea<sub>1</sub></i>) and Na<sup>+</sup> desolvation (<i>Ea<sub>2</sub></i>) are calculated by the classical Arrhenius equation in Figure S44 and Table S5–S7.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400539-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400539-bib-0043_R_d249474977e1051" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Mater.">20</a></sup></span> The turning point of fitted <i>E</i><sub>a</sub> curves results from the varied solvation structures at different temperatures, which has been demonstrated by salt precipitation, as shown in low-temperature Raman spectra. The N-mixTHF has the lowest <i>Ea<sub>1</sub></i> of 8.17 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup> compared to 61.24 and 54.99 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup> in N-THF and N-2MeTHF, respectively, as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202400539-fig-0004">4b</a>. Inorganic-rich SEI characterized by high ionic conductivity has low interfacial impedance to promote the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion. N-mixTHF exhibits a smaller <i>Ea<sub>2</sub></i> value of 16.60 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup> than that in N-THF (52.94 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>) and N-2MeTHF (41.21 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>), as displayed in Figure <a href="#anie202400539-fig-0004">4c</a>. This is attributed to the decreased Na<sup>+</sup>-dipole interactions in N-mixTHF, which reduce the binding strength between Na<sup>+</sup> and solvents, leading to facilitated Na<sup>+</sup> desolvation on the HC anode. The addition of 2MeTHF simultaneously decreases the energy barrier for Na<sup>+</sup> desolvation and Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion through the SEI to enhance the anode interfacial kinetics.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202400539-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8996291f-bddb-4866-9526-a15e02b2fb8b/anie202400539-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/8996291f-bddb-4866-9526-a15e02b2fb8b/anie202400539-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/0b3b9a7a-e918-49af-b5d5-81111425aec4/anie202400539-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8996291f-bddb-4866-9526-a15e02b2fb8b/anie202400539-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202400539-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202400539" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>(a) Nyquist plots of Na||Na batteries after fifty cycles of plating and stripping in N-mixTHF (from 193 to 323 K), N-2MeTHF (from 233 to 323 K), and N-THF (from 243 to 323 K). (b) Activation energy of Na<sup>+</sup> transport through SEI (<i>Ea<sub>1</sub></i>) and (c) Na<sup>+</sup> desolvation (<i>Ea<sub>2</sub></i>) derived from Nyquist plots. (d) <i>t</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> in three electrolytes at 25 and −40 °C. (e) GITT curves of HC anodes in three electrolytes at −40 °C.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>The Na<sup>+</sup> transference number (<i>t<sub>Na</sub></i><sup><i>+</i></sup>) of N-mixTHF decreases from 0.54 to 0.49 in the range of 25 to −40 °C, higher than that of N-THF (from 0.39 to 0.15) and N-2MeTHF (from 0.23 to 0.21), as displayed in Figure <a href="#anie202400539-fig-0004">4d</a> and Table S8. This is ascribed to the weakened Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent interactions in N-mixTHF, contributing to higher Na<sup>+</sup> mobility in the electrolyte.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400539-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400539-bib-0044_R_d249474977e1156" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Mater.">21</a></sup></span> Differential capacity analysis (<i>dQ/dV</i>) curves were measured to investigate interfacial reactions in different electrolytes. N-mixTHF exhibits an increased reduction current at ~1.11 V, which is 40 mV higher than that of N-THF and N-2MeTHF, as shown in Figure S45–S46. This demonstrates more preferential anion decomposition in N-mixTHF, facilitating inorganic-rich SEI formation on the anode.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400539-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400539-bib-0045_R_d249474977e1161" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">22</a></sup></span></p>
            
            <p>The galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) is conducted to estimate Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients (<i>D</i><sub><i>Na+</i></sub>) and ion transport kinetic in different electrolytes at room and low temperatures. The average <i>D</i><sub><i>Na+</i></sub> of the HC anode in N-mixTHF, N-2MeTHF, and N-THF range from 10<sup>−12.75</sup> to 10<sup>−11.55</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>. This indicates similar Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion in the HC anodes of the three electrolytes at 25 °C, as shown in Figure S47–S48. However, N-mixTHF has higher <i>D</i><sub><i>Na+</i></sub> ranged from 10<sup>−12.93</sup> to 10<sup>−12.13</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> at −40 °C, compared with that in N-THF (10<sup>−14.95–</sup>10<sup>−14.20</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>) and N-2MeTHF (10<sup>−13.64–</sup>10<sup>−13.14</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>), as depicted in Figure <a href="#anie202400539-fig-0004">4e</a> and Figure S49. The faster Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion in N-mixTHF at low temperatures is due to the weakened ion-dipole interaction and enhanced interfacial kinetics on the HC anode.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202400539-sec-0005">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202400539-sec-0005-title"> Electrochemical Performance</h3>
            
            <p>The electrochemical performance of the HC anode in N-mixTHF, N-2MeTHF, and N-THF are evaluated in the Na-HC half cells between 0.01 and 1.5 V. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202400539-fig-0005">5a</a>, the HC anodes deliver similarly capacity of 270.3, 267.8, and 269.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup> at 25 °C in N-mixTHF, N-2MeTHF, and N-THF, respectively. This demonstrates reversible Na storage of HC anode in the three electrolytes, as shown in the CV results in Figure S50. The HC anode exhibits superior rate capability in N-mixTHF, which has the highest reversible capacity of 192.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 1000 mA g<sup>−1</sup> in Figure <a href="#anie202400539-fig-0005">5b</a>, compared to that of 150.2 and 56.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> in N-THF and N-2MeTHF, respectively. Figure S51 displays the cycling stability of the HC anode in three electrolytes at different current densities at 25 °C. The HC anode in N-mixTHF presents superior cycling stability with capacity retention of 80.8 % over 3600 cycles and an average Coulombic efficiency (CE) of 99.9 %. The improved battery performance in N-mixTHF benefits from the Na<sup>+</sup>-dipole regulation and stable anion-derived SEI construction.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202400539-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/bf2fdc10-5ca4-45d9-99e7-5b9523645ccd/anie202400539-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/bf2fdc10-5ca4-45d9-99e7-5b9523645ccd/anie202400539-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d03dda71-9281-4304-91b9-d71f7d2fd622/anie202400539-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/bf2fdc10-5ca4-45d9-99e7-5b9523645ccd/anie202400539-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202400539-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202400539" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>(a) Charge/discharge curves of HC in N-mixTHF, N-2MeTHF, and N-THF at 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup> in first cycle at 25 °C. (b) Rate capability. (c) Charge/discharge curves of HC at 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup> in first cycle at −40 °C, and (d) Long cycling performance in N-mixTHF at different temperatures (100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> beyond −20 °C, 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup> at −40 °C, and −60 °C). (e) Cycle performance of full cells at 25 °C (200 mA g<sup>−1</sup>) and −40 °C (50 mA g<sup>−1</sup>).</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>The low-temperature electrochemical performance of HC anode in different electrolytes at −40 °C is shown in Figure <a href="#anie202400539-fig-0005">5c</a> and Figure S52. A highly reversible capacity of 243.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> is achieved in N-mixTHF at 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup> at −40 °C, compared to 44.5 and 120.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> in N-THF and N-2MeTHF, respectively. N-mixTHF exhibits superior cycling stability with 95.2 % capacity retention after 100 cycles at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> at −40 °C. Figure <a href="#anie202400539-fig-0005">5d</a> and Figure S53 display the electrochemical behavior of the HC anode in N-mixTHF from −80 to 55 °C. The HC anode delivers a high specific capacity of 205.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at −60 °C, maintaining 76.1 % of the capacity obtained at 25 °C. A reversible specific capacity of 58.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> can be achieved in N-mixTHF even at −80 °C. Furthermore, the HC anode based on N-mixTHF obtains a high capacity retention of more than 95 % and an average CE of 99.9 % after 100 cycles at the temperature range from −80 to 55 °C, suggesting excellent adaptability of N-mixTHF in the high and low temperatures. This superior performance is attributed to weakened ion-dipole interactions and anion-enhanced solvation structures, which facilitate Na<sup>+</sup> desolvation and inorganic-rich SEI formation on the anode, resulting in better performances than previously reported low-temperature electrolytes for SIBs, as listed in Table S9. Full cells coupled with Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) cathodes and HC anodes are evaluated in Figure <a href="#anie202400539-fig-0005">5e</a> and Figure S54–S57. The HC||NVP full cells exhibit high specific capacity of 100.4 and 61.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, with superior capacity retention of ~82.5 % and ~100 % after 250 cycles at 25 and −40 °C, respectively. These outstanding electrochemical performances indicate the promising application of the WSE in SIBs over a wide temperature range.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202400539-sec-0006">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202400539-sec-0006-title"> Conclusions</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, the weakened ion-dipole interactions are realized in the electrolyte N-mixTHF with 2MeTHF and THF as cosolvents for stable sodium storage in HC anodes at low temperatures. Decreased cation-solvent coordination leads to more anions in the first solvation sheath to endow N-mixTHF with a low freezing point of −83.3 °C. MD simulations demonstrate that anion-enhanced solvation structures are consistently maintained at low temperatures. This favors a reduced desolvation energy of 16.60 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup> in N-mixTHF, compared to 52.94 and 41.21 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup> in N-THF and N-2MeTHF, respectively. The weakened ion-dipole interactions in N-mixTHF contribute to the preferential PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> decomposition on anodes. The generated NaF-rich SEI provides low Na<sup>+</sup> transport barrier of 8.17 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>, facilitating interfacial kinetics in N-mixTHF. This enables Na||HC half cells with N-mixTHF to afford reversible capacity of 243.2 and 205.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (at 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup>) at −40 and −60 °C, respectively. The full cell paired with NVP operates over 250 cycles with high capacity retention of ~100 % at 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup> at −40 °C. This work highlights the role of ion-dipole interactions in electrolyte modulation for ultra-low temperature batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
